<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">chemical element with atomic number 95</div><table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption><span style="white-space:nowrap">Americium,  <sub><span style="font-size:smaller;">95</span></sub>Am</span></caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Americium_microscope.jpg"><img alt="Americium microscope.jpg" data-file-height="981" data-file-width="1024" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Americium_microscope.jpg/220px-Americium_microscope.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Americium_microscope.jpg/330px-Americium_microscope.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Americium_microscope.jpg/440px-Americium_microscope.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#ff99cc">General properties</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Pronunciation</th><td>
<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span></span>/</a></span></span> <wbr/>​<span class="nowrap">(<a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">AM</span>-ə-<span style="font-size:90%">RISS</span>-ee-əm</i></a>)</span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Appearance</th><td>
silvery white</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Mass_number" title="Mass number">Mass number</a></th><td>

<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_americium" title="Template:Infobox americium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_americium" title="Template talk:Infobox americium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_americium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div> | <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Americium</b> is a synthetic <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Am</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 95. It is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transuranic_element" title="Transuranic element">transuranic</a> member of the <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinide</a> series, in the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a> located under the <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanide</a> element <a href="/wiki/Europium" title="Europium">europium</a>, and thus by analogy was named after the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Americium was first produced in 1944 by the group of <a href="/wiki/Glenn_T._Seaborg" title="Glenn T. Seaborg">Glenn T. Seaborg</a> from <a href="/wiki/Berkeley,_California" title="Berkeley, California">Berkeley, California</a>, at the <a href="/wiki/Metallurgical_Laboratory" title="Metallurgical Laboratory">Metallurgical Laboratory</a> of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a>, a part of the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>. Although it is the third element in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transuranic_element" title="Transuranic element">transuranic series</a>, it was discovered fourth, after the heavier <a href="/wiki/Curium" title="Curium">curium</a>. The discovery was kept secret and only released to the public in November 1945. Most americium is produced by <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> being bombarded with <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a> – one <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonne</a> of spent <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel" title="Nuclear fuel">nuclear fuel</a> contains about 100 grams of americium. It is widely used in commercial <a href="/wiki/Ionization_chamber" title="Ionization chamber">ionization chamber</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smoke_detectors" title="Smoke detectors">smoke detectors</a>, as well as in <a href="/wiki/Neutron_source" title="Neutron source">neutron sources</a> and industrial gauges. Several unusual applications, such as nuclear batteries or fuel for space ships with nuclear propulsion, have been proposed for the <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> <sup>242m</sup>Am, but they are as yet hindered by the scarcity and high price of this <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">nuclear isomer</a>.
</p><p>Americium is a relatively soft <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive</a> metal with silvery appearance. Its  <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_americium" title="Isotopes of americium">common isotopes</a> are <sup>241</sup>Am and <sup>243</sup>Am. In chemical compounds, americium usually assumes the <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a> +3, especially in solutions. Several other oxidation states are known, which range from +2 to +7 and can be identified by their characteristic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_absorption" title="Optical absorption">optical absorption</a> spectra. The crystal lattice of solid americium and its compounds contain small intrinsic radiogenic defects, due to <a href="/wiki/Metamictization" title="Metamictization">metamictization</a> induced by self-irradiation with alpha particles, which accumulates with time; this can cause a drift of some material properties over time, more noticeable in older samples.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2409" data-file-width="3000" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg/220px-Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg/330px-Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg/440px-Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 60-inch cyclotron at the Lawrence Radiation Laboratory, <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">University of California, Berkeley</a>, in August 1939.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Americium.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="378" data-file-width="250" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/dc/Americium.jpg/170px-Americium.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/dc/Americium.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Americium.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The triangle in the glass tube contains the first sample of americium (as the hydroxide (Am(OH)<sub id="cite_ref-3">3</sub>)), produced in 1944.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Although americium was likely produced in previous nuclear experiments, it was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discoveries_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Discoveries of the chemical elements">first intentionally synthesized</a>, isolated and identified in late autumn 1944, at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">University of California, Berkeley</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Glenn_T._Seaborg" title="Glenn T. Seaborg">Glenn T. Seaborg</a>, Leon O. Morgan, Ralph A. James, and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Ghiorso" title="Albert Ghiorso">Albert Ghiorso</a>. They used a 60-inch <a href="/wiki/Cyclotron" title="Cyclotron">cyclotron</a> at the University of California, Berkeley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The element was chemically identified at the Metallurgical Laboratory (now <a href="/wiki/Argonne_National_Laboratory" title="Argonne National Laboratory">Argonne National Laboratory</a>) of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a>. Following the lighter <a href="/wiki/Neptunium" title="Neptunium">neptunium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>, and heavier <a href="/wiki/Curium" title="Curium">curium</a>, americium was the fourth transuranium element to be discovered. At the time, the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a> had been restructured by Seaborg to its present layout, containing the actinide row below the <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanide</a> one. This led to americium being located right below its twin lanthanide element europium; it was thus by analogy named after the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>: "The name americium (after the Americas) and the symbol Am are suggested for the element on the basis of its position as the sixth member of the actinide rare-earth series, analogous to europium, Eu, of the lanthanide series."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g1252_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-g1252-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The new element was isolated from its <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxides</a> in a complex, multi-step process. First <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>-239 nitrate (<sup>239</sup>PuNO<sub>3</sub>) solution was coated on a <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> foil of about 0.5 cm<sup>2</sup> area, the solution was evaporated and the residue was converted into plutonium dioxide (PuO<sub>2</sub>) by annealing. After cyclotron irradiation, the coating was dissolved with <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a>, and then precipitated as the hydroxide using concentrated aqueous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ammonium_hydroxide" title="Ammonium hydroxide">ammonia solution</a>. The residue was dissolved in <a href="/wiki/Perchloric_acid" title="Perchloric acid">perchloric acid</a>. Further separation was carried out by <a href="/wiki/Ion_exchange" title="Ion exchange">ion exchange</a>, yielding a certain isotope of curium. The separation of curium and americium was so painstaking that those elements were initially called by the Berkeley group as <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/pandemonium" title="wikt:pandemonium">pandemonium</a></i> (from Greek for <i>all demons</i> or <i>hell</i>) and <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/delirium" title="wikt:delirium">delirium</a></i> (from Latin for <i>madness</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-radio_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-radio-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Initial experiments yielded four americium isotopes: <sup>241</sup>Am, <sup>242</sup>Am, <sup>239</sup>Am and <sup>238</sup>Am. <a href="/wiki/Americium-241" title="Americium-241">Americium-241</a> was directly obtained from plutonium upon absorption of two neutrons. It decays by emission of a <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">α-particle</a> to <sup>237</sup>Np; the <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of this decay was first determined as <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002510000000000000♠</span>510<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>20</span> years but then corrected to 432.2 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nubase_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-nubase-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>

</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {^{239}_{94}Pu -&gt;[{\ce {(n,\gamma)}}] ^{240}_{94}Pu -&gt;[{\ce {(n,\gamma)}}] ^{241}_{94}Pu -&gt;[\beta^-][14.35\ {\ce {yr}}] ^{241}_{95}Am}}\ \left({\ce {-&gt;[\alpha][432.2\ {\ce {yr}}] ^{237}_{93}Np}}\right)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c1e04227243220777cfab356aab3d5ea28566de2" style="vertical-align: -2.208ex; margin-top: -0.415ex; margin-bottom: -0.464ex; width:61.505ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<dl><dd><small> The times are <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-lives</a></small></dd></dl>
<p>The second isotope <sup>242</sup>Am was produced upon neutron bombardment of the already-created <sup>241</sup>Am. Upon rapid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta-decay" title="Beta-decay">β-decay</a>, <sup>242</sup>Am converts into the isotope of curium <sup>242</sup>Cm (which had been discovered previously). The half-life of this decay was initially determined at 17 hours, which was close to the presently accepted value of 16.02 h.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nubase_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-nubase-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {^{241}_{95}Am -&gt;[{\ce {(n,\gamma)}}] ^{242}_{95}Am}}\ \left({\ce {-&gt;[\beta^-][16.02\ {\ce {h}}] ^{242}_{96}Cm}}\right)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {^{241}_{95}Am -&gt;[{\ce {(n,\gamma)}}] ^{242}_{95}Am}}\ \left({\ce {-&gt;[\beta^-][16.02\ {\ce {h}}] ^{242}_{96}Cm}}\right)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {^{241}_{95}Am -&gt;[{\ce {(n,\gamma)}}] ^{242}_{95}Am}}\ \left({\ce {-&gt;[\beta^-][16.02\ {\ce {h}}] ^{242}_{96}Cm}}\right)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5f7ea91b21dda64052123dd4daa460996dd08332" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; margin-top: -0.415ex; width:36.647ex; height:6.343ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>The discovery of americium and curium in 1944 was closely related to the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>; the results were confidential and declassified only in 1945. Seaborg leaked the synthesis of the elements 95 and 96 on the U.S. radio show for children <i><a href="/wiki/Quiz_Kids" title="Quiz Kids">Quiz Kids</a></i> five days before the official presentation at an <a href="/wiki/American_Chemical_Society" title="American Chemical Society">American Chemical Society</a> meeting on 11 November 1945, when one of the listeners asked whether any new transuranium element beside plutonium and neptunium had been discovered during the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-radio_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-radio-9">[9]</a></sup> After the discovery of americium isotopes <sup>241</sup>Am and <sup>242</sup>Am, their production and compounds were patented listing only Seaborg as the inventor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The initial americium samples weighed a few micrograms; they were barely visible and were identified by their radioactivity. The first substantial amounts of metallic americium weighing 40–200 micrograms were not prepared until 1951 by reduction of <a href="/wiki/Americium(III)_fluoride" title="Americium(III) fluoride">americium(III) fluoride</a> with <a href="/wiki/Barium" title="Barium">barium</a> metal in high vacuum at 1100 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM_METALL1_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-AM_METALL1-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">Nuclear reprocessing</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ivy_Mike_-_mushroom_cloud.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="637" data-file-width="800" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Ivy_Mike_-_mushroom_cloud.jpg/220px-Ivy_Mike_-_mushroom_cloud.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Ivy_Mike_-_mushroom_cloud.jpg/330px-Ivy_Mike_-_mushroom_cloud.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Ivy_Mike_-_mushroom_cloud.jpg/440px-Ivy_Mike_-_mushroom_cloud.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ivy_Mike_-_mushroom_cloud.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Americium was detected in the fallout from the <i><a href="/wiki/Ivy_Mike" title="Ivy Mike">Ivy Mike</a></i> nuclear test.</div></div></div>
<p>The longest-lived and most common isotopes of americium, <sup>241</sup>Am and <sup>243</sup>Am, have half-lives of 432.2 and 7,370 years, respectively. Therefore, any <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">primordial</a> americium (americium that was present on Earth during its formation) should have decayed by now.
</p><p>Existing americium is concentrated in the areas used for the atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_testing" title="Nuclear weapons testing">nuclear weapons tests</a> conducted between 1945 and 1980, as well as at the sites of nuclear incidents, such as the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl disaster</a>. For example, the analysis of the debris at the testing site of the first U.S. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bomb" title="Hydrogen bomb">hydrogen bomb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ivy_Mike" title="Ivy Mike">Ivy Mike</a>, (1 November 1952, <a href="/wiki/Enewetak_Atoll" title="Enewetak Atoll">Enewetak Atoll</a>), revealed high concentrations of various actinides including americium;  but due to military secrecy, this result was not published until later, in 1956.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Trinitite" title="Trinitite">Trinitite</a>, the glassy residue left on the desert floor near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alamogordo" title="Alamogordo">Alamogordo</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>, after the <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>-based <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trinity_test" title="Trinity test">Trinity</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_testing" title="Nuclear testing">nuclear bomb test</a> on 16 July 1945, contains traces of americium-241. Elevated levels of americium were also detected at the <a href="/wiki/1968_Thule_Air_Base_B-52_crash" title="1968 Thule Air Base B-52 crash">crash site</a> of a US <a href="/wiki/Boeing_B-52_Stratofortress" title="Boeing B-52 Stratofortress">Boeing B-52</a> bomber aircraft, which carried four hydrogen bombs, in 1968 in <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>In other regions, the average radioactivity of surface soil due to residual americium is only about 0.01 <a href="/wiki/Curie" title="Curie">picocuries</a>/g (0.37 <a href="/wiki/Becquerel" title="Becquerel">mBq</a>/g). Atmospheric americium compounds are poorly soluble in common solvents and mostly adhere to soil particles. Soil analysis revealed about 1,900 times higher concentration of americium inside sandy soil particles than in the water present in the soil pores; an even higher ratio was measured in <a href="/wiki/Loam" title="Loam">loam</a> soils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-am_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-am-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Americium is produced mostly artificially in small quantities, for research purposes. A tonne of spent nuclear fuel contains about 100 grams of various americium isotopes, mostly <sup>241</sup>Am and <sup>243</sup>Am.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Their prolonged radioactivity is undesirable for the disposal, and therefore americium, together with other long-lived actinides, must be neutralized. The associated procedure may involve several steps, where americium is first separated and then converted by neutron bombardment in special reactors to short-lived nuclides. This procedure is well known as <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">nuclear transmutation</a>, but it is still being developed for americium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transuranic_element" title="Transuranic element">transuranic elements</a> from americium to <a href="/wiki/Fermium" title="Fermium">fermium</a> occurred naturally in the <a href="/wiki/Natural_nuclear_fission_reactor" title="Natural nuclear fission reactor">natural nuclear fission reactor</a> at <a href="/wiki/Oklo" title="Oklo">Oklo</a>, but no longer do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Synthesis_and_extraction">Synthesis and extraction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Synthesis and extraction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotope_nucleosyntheses">Isotope nucleosyntheses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Isotope nucleosyntheses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elutionskurven_Tb_Gd_Eu_und_Bk_Cm_Am.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1692" data-file-width="1218" height="306" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Elutionskurven_Tb_Gd_Eu_und_Bk_Cm_Am.png/220px-Elutionskurven_Tb_Gd_Eu_und_Bk_Cm_Am.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Elutionskurven_Tb_Gd_Eu_und_Bk_Cm_Am.png/330px-Elutionskurven_Tb_Gd_Eu_und_Bk_Cm_Am.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Elutionskurven_Tb_Gd_Eu_und_Bk_Cm_Am.png/440px-Elutionskurven_Tb_Gd_Eu_und_Bk_Cm_Am.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elutionskurven_Tb_Gd_Eu_und_Bk_Cm_Am.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Chromatography" title="Chromatography">Chromatographic</a> <a href="/wiki/Elution" title="Elution">elution</a> curves revealing the similarity between the lanthanides Tb, Gd, and Eu and the corresponding actinides Bk, Cm, and Am.</div></div></div>
<p>Americium has been produced in small quantities in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a> for decades, and kilograms of its <sup>241</sup>Am and <sup>243</sup>Am isotopes have been accumulated by now.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g1262_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-g1262-23">[23]</a></sup> Nevertheless, since it was first offered for sale in 1962, its price, about 1,500 <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">USD</a> per gram of <sup>241</sup>Am, remains almost unchanged owing to the very complex separation procedure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smoke_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-smoke-24">[24]</a></sup> The heavier isotope <sup>243</sup>Am is produced in much smaller amounts; it is thus more difficult to separate, resulting in a higher cost of the order 100,000–160,000 USD/g.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Americium is not synthesized directly from uranium – the most common reactor material – but from the plutonium isotope <sup>239</sup>Pu. The latter needs to be produced first, according to the following nuclear process:
</p>

<p>The capture of two neutrons by <sup>239</sup>Pu (a so-called (n,γ) reaction), followed by a β-decay, results in <sup>241</sup>Am:
</p>

</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {^{239}_{94}Pu -&gt;[{\ce {2(n,\gamma)}}] ^{241}_{94}Pu -&gt;[\beta^-][14.35 \ {\ce {yr}}] ^{241}_{95}Am}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7388c94ee2af7cf763dc4372225ef135072e2b16" style="vertical-align: -2.097ex; margin-top: -0.415ex; margin-bottom: -0.574ex; width:33.169ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>The plutonium present in spent nuclear fuel contains about 12% of <sup>241</sup>Pu. Because it spontaneously converts to <sup>241</sup>Am, <sup>241</sup>Pu can be extracted and may be used to generate further <sup>241</sup>Am.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smoke_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-smoke-24">[24]</a></sup> However, this process is rather slow: half of the original amount of <sup>241</sup>Pu decays to <sup>241</sup>Am after about 15 years, and the <sup>241</sup>Am amount reaches a maximum after 70 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The obtained <sup>241</sup>Am can be used for generating heavier americium isotopes by further neutron capture inside a nuclear reactor. In a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_water_reactor" title="Light water reactor">light water reactor</a> (LWR), 79% of <sup>241</sup>Am converts to <sup>242</sup>Am and 10% to its <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">nuclear isomer</a> <sup>242m</sup>Am:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[note 1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p>

<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Americium-242" title="Americium-242">Americium-242</a> has a half-life of only 16 hours, which makes its further up-conversion to <sup>243</sup>Am, extremely inefficient. The latter isotope is produced instead in a process where <sup>239</sup>Pu captures four neutrons under high <a href="/wiki/Neutron_flux" title="Neutron flux">neutron flux</a>:

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metal_generation">Metal generation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Metal generation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most synthesis routines yield a mixture of different actinide isotopes in oxide forms, from which isotopes of americium can be separated. In a typical procedure, the spent reactor fuel (e.g. <a href="/wiki/MOX_fuel" title="MOX fuel">MOX fuel</a>) is dissolved in <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a>, and the bulk of uranium and plutonium is removed using a <a href="/wiki/PUREX" title="PUREX">PUREX</a>-type extraction (<b>P</b>lutonium–<b>UR</b>anium <b>EX</b>traction) with <a href="/wiki/Tributyl_phosphate" title="Tributyl phosphate">tributyl phosphate</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a>. The lanthanides and remaining actinides are then separated from the aqueous residue (<a href="/wiki/Raffinate" title="Raffinate">raffinate</a>) by a <a href="/wiki/Diamide" title="Diamide">diamide</a>-based extraction, to give, after stripping, a mixture of trivalent actinides and lanthanides. Americium compounds are then selectively extracted using multi-step <a href="/wiki/Chromatography" title="Chromatography">chromatographic</a> and centrifugation techniques<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> with an appropriate reagent. A large amount of work has been done on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solvent_extraction" title="Solvent extraction">solvent extraction</a> of americium. For example, a 2003 <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU</a>-funded project codenamed "EUROPART" studied <a href="/wiki/Triazine" title="Triazine">triazines</a> and other compounds as potential extraction agents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/BTBP" title="BTBP"><i>bis</i>-triazinyl bipyridine</a> complex was proposed in 2009 as such a reagent is highly selective to americium (and curium).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> Separation of americium from the highly similar curium can be achieved by treating a slurry of their hydroxides in aqueous <a href="/wiki/Sodium_bicarbonate" title="Sodium bicarbonate">sodium bicarbonate</a> with <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a>, at elevated temperatures. Both Am and Cm are mostly present in solutions in the +3 valence state; whereas curium remains unchanged, americium oxidizes to soluble Am(IV) complexes which can be washed away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Metallic americium is obtained by <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reduction</a> from its compounds. <a href="/wiki/Americium(III)_fluoride" title="Americium(III) fluoride">Americium(III) fluoride</a> was first used for this purpose. The reaction was conducted using elemental <a href="/wiki/Barium" title="Barium">barium</a> as reducing agent in a water- and oxygen-free environment inside an apparatus made of <a href="/wiki/Tantalum" title="Tantalum">tantalum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM_METALL1_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-AM_METALL1-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gmelin_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gmelin-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p3_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-p3-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathrm {2\ AmF_{3}\ +\ 3\ Ba\ \longrightarrow \ 2\ Am\ +\ 3\ BaF_{2}} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>An alternative is the reduction of <a href="/wiki/Americium_dioxide" title="Americium dioxide">americium dioxide</a> by metallic <a href="/wiki/Lanthanum" title="Lanthanum">lanthanum</a> or <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p3_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-p3-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM_METALL2_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-AM_METALL2-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Closest_packing_ABAC.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1299" data-file-width="1417" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Closest_packing_ABAC.png/220px-Closest_packing_ABAC.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Closest_packing_ABAC.png/330px-Closest_packing_ABAC.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Closest_packing_ABAC.png/440px-Closest_packing_ABAC.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Closest_packing_ABAC.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Double-hexagonal close packing with the layer sequence ABAC in the crystal structure of α-americium (A: green, B: blue, C: red).</div></div></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>, americium is located to the right of plutonium, to the left of curium, and below the lanthanide <a href="/wiki/Europium" title="Europium">europium</a>, with which it shares many similarities in physical and chemical properties. Americium is a highly radioactive element. When freshly prepared, it has a silvery-white metallic lustre, but then slowly tarnishes in air. With a density of 12 g/cm<sup>3</sup>, americium is less dense than both curium (13.52 g/cm<sup>3</sup>) and plutonium (19.8 g/cm<sup>3</sup>); but has a higher density than europium (5.264 g/cm<sup>3</sup>)—mostly because of its higher atomic mass. Americium is relatively soft and easily deformable and has a significantly lower <a href="/wiki/Bulk_modulus" title="Bulk modulus">bulk modulus</a> than the actinides before it: Th, Pa, U, Np and Pu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pressure_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-pressure-41">[40]</a></sup> Its melting point of 1173 °C is significantly higher than that of plutonium (639 °C) and europium (826 °C), but lower than for curium (1340 °C).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM_METALL2_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-AM_METALL2-40">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM_METALL2_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-AM_METALL2-40">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM_METALL4_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-AM_METALL4-42">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>At ambient conditions, americium is present in its most stable α form which has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hexagonal_crystal_system" title="Hexagonal crystal system">hexagonal crystal symmetry</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Space_group" title="Space group">space group</a> P6<sub>3</sub>/mmc with lattice parameters <i>a</i> = 346.8 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Picometer" title="Picometer">pm</a> and <i>c</i> = 1124 pm, and four atoms per <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unit_cell" title="Unit cell">unit cell</a>. The crystal consists of a double-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Close-packing_of_spheres" title="Close-packing of spheres">hexagonal close packing</a> with the layer sequence ABAC and so is isotypic with α-lanthanum and several actinides such as α-curium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gmelin_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gmelin-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM_METALL4_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-AM_METALL4-42">[41]</a></sup> The crystal structure of americium changes with pressure and temperature. When compressed at room temperature to 5 GPa, α-Am transforms to the β modification, which has a <a href="/wiki/Cubic_crystal_system" title="Cubic crystal system">face-centered cubic</a> (<i>fcc</i>) symmetry, space group Fm<span style="text-decoration:overline;">3</span>m and lattice constant <i>a</i> = 489 pm. This <i>fcc</i> structure is equivalent to the closest packing with the sequence ABC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gmelin_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gmelin-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM_METALL4_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-AM_METALL4-42">[41]</a></sup> Upon further compression to 23 GPa, americium transforms to an <a href="/wiki/Orthorhombic_crystal_system" title="Orthorhombic crystal system">orthorhombic</a> γ-Am structure similar to that of α-uranium. There are no further transitions observed up to 52 GPa, except for an appearance of a monoclinic phase at pressures between 10 and 15 GPa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pressure_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-pressure-41">[40]</a></sup> There is no consistency on the status of this phase in the literature, which also sometimes lists the α, β and γ phases as I, II and III. The β-γ transition is accompanied by a 6% decrease in the crystal volume; although theory also predicts a significant volume change for the α-β transition, it is not observed experimentally. The pressure of the α-β transition decreases with increasing temperature, and when α-americium is heated at ambient pressure, at 770 °C it changes into an <i>fcc</i> phase which is different from β-Am, and at 1075 °C it converts to a <a href="/wiki/Cubic_crystal_system" title="Cubic crystal system">body-centered cubic</a> structure. The pressure-temperature phase diagram of americium is thus rather similar to those of lanthanum, <a href="/wiki/Praseodymium" title="Praseodymium">praseodymium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neodymium" title="Neodymium">neodymium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>As with many other actinides, self-damage of the crystal lattice due to alpha-particle irradiation is intrinsic to americium. It is especially noticeable at low temperatures, where the mobility of the produced <a href="/wiki/Interstitial_defect" title="Interstitial defect">lattice defects</a> is relatively low, by broadening of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_diffraction" title="X-ray diffraction">X-ray diffraction</a> peaks. This effect makes somewhat uncertain the temperature of americium and some of its properties, such as electrical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resistivity" title="Resistivity">resistivity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> So for americium-241, the resistivity at 4.2 K increases with time from about 2 µOhm·cm to 10 µOhm·cm after 40 hours, and saturates at about 16 µOhm·cm after 140 hours. This effect is less pronounced at room temperature, due to annihilation of radiation defects; also heating to room temperature the sample which was kept for hours at low temperatures restores its resistivity. In fresh samples, the resistivity gradually increases with temperature from about 2 µOhm·cm at <a href="/wiki/Liquid_helium" title="Liquid helium">liquid helium</a> to 69 µOhm·cm at room temperature; this behavior is similar to that of neptunium, uranium, thorium and <a href="/wiki/Protactinium" title="Protactinium">protactinium</a>, but is different from plutonium and curium which show a rapid rise up to 60 K followed by saturation. The room temperature value for americium is lower than that of neptunium, plutonium and curium, but higher than for uranium, thorium and protactinium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-res_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-res-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Americium is <a href="/wiki/Paramagnetism" title="Paramagnetism">paramagnetic</a> in a wide temperature range, from that of <a href="/wiki/Liquid_helium" title="Liquid helium">liquid helium</a>, to room temperature and above. This behavior is markedly different from that of its neighbor curium which exhibits antiferromagnetic transition at 52 K.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">thermal expansion</a> coefficient of americium is slightly anisotropic and amounts to <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6994749999999999999♠</span>(7.5<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.2)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−6</sup> /°C</span> along the shorter <i>a</i> axis and <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6994620000000000000♠</span>(6.2<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.4)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−6</sup> /°C</span> for the longer <i>c</i> hexagonal axis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM_METALL4_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-AM_METALL4-42">[41]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Enthalpy_change_of_solution" title="Enthalpy change of solution">enthalpy of dissolution</a> of americium metal in <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> at standard conditions is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">2994379400000000000♠</span>−620.6<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>1.3 kJ/mol</span>, from which the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_enthalpy_change_of_formation_(data_table)" title="Standard enthalpy change of formation (data table)">standard enthalpy change of formation</a> (Δ<sub>f</sub><i>H</i>°) of aqueous Am<sup>3+</sup> ion is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">2994378800000000000♠</span>−621.2<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>2.0 kJ/mol</span>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_potential" title="Standard potential">standard potential</a> Am<sup>3+</sup>/Am<sup>0</sup> is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">2999792000000000000♠</span>−2.08<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.01 V</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_properties">Chemical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Chemical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Americium34.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="284" data-file-width="350" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/ba/Americium34.jpg/220px-Americium34.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/ba/Americium34.jpg/330px-Americium34.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/ba/Americium34.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Americium34.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Americium ions in solution: Am<sup>3+</sup> (left) and Am<sup>4+</sup> (right). Am<sup>3+</sup> is colorless at low and reddish at higher concentrations.</div></div></div>
<p>Americium readily reacts with oxygen and dissolves well in <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a>. The most common <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a> for americium is +3,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p4_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-p4-47">[46]</a></sup> in which americium compounds are rather stable against oxidation and reduction. In this sense, americium is chemically similar to most lanthanides. The trivalent americium forms insoluble <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluoride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxalate" title="Oxalate">oxalate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iodate" title="Iodate">iodate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> and other salts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p4_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-p4-47">[46]</a></sup> Other oxidation states have been observed between +2 and +7, which is the widest range among the actinide elements. Their color in aqueous solutions varies as follows: Am<sup>3+</sup> (colorless to yellow-reddish), Am<sup>4+</sup> (yellow-reddish), Am<sup>V</sup><span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>; (yellow), Am<sup>VI</sup><span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> (brown) and Am<sup>VII</sup><span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> (dark green).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g1265_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-g1265-49">[48]</a></sup> All oxidation states have their characteristic optical absorption spectra, with a few sharp peaks in the visible and mid-infrared regions, and the position and intensity of these peaks can be converted into the concentrations of the corresponding oxidation states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amoh4_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-amoh4-51">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carbonate_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-carbonate-52">[51]</a></sup> For example, Am(III) has two sharp peaks at 504 and 811 nm, Am(V) at 514 and 715 nm, and Am(VI) at 666 and 992 nm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haxav_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-haxav-53">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Americium compounds with oxidation state +4 and higher are strong oxidizing agents, comparable in strength to the <a href="/wiki/Permanganate" title="Permanganate">permanganate</a> ion (<span class="chemf nowrap">MnO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>) in acidic solutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HOWI_1956_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-HOWI_1956-54">[53]</a></sup> Whereas the Am<sup>4+</sup> ions are unstable in solutions and readily convert to Am<sup>3+</sup>, the +4 oxidation state occurs well in solids, such as <a href="/wiki/Americium_dioxide" title="Americium dioxide">americium dioxide</a> (AmO<sub>2</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Americium(IV)_fluoride" title="Americium(IV) fluoride">americium(IV) fluoride</a> (AmF<sub>4</sub>).
</p><p>All <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pentavalent" title="Pentavalent">pentavalent</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hexavalent" title="Hexavalent">hexavalent</a> americium compounds are complex salts such as KAmO<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>, Li<sub>3</sub>AmO<sub>4</sub> and Li<sub>6</sub>AmO<sub>6</sub>, Ba<sub>3</sub>AmO<sub>6</sub>, AmO<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>. These high oxidation states Am(IV), Am(V) and Am(VI) can be prepared from Am(III) by oxidation with <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_persulfate" title="Ammonium persulfate">ammonium persulfate</a> in dilute nitric acid,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> with <a href="/wiki/Silver_oxide" title="Silver oxide">silver(I) oxide</a> in <a href="/wiki/Perchloric_acid" title="Perchloric acid">perchloric acid</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haxav_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-haxav-53">[52]</a></sup> or with <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sodium_persulfate" title="Sodium persulfate">sodium persulfate</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sodium_carbonate" title="Sodium carbonate">sodium carbonate</a> solutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carbonate_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-carbonate-52">[51]</a></sup> The pentavalent oxidation state of americium was first observed in 1951.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> It is present in aqueous solution in the form of <span class="chemf nowrap">AmO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> ions (acidic) or <span class="chemf nowrap">AmO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> ions (alkaline) which are however unstable and subject to several rapid <a href="/wiki/Disproportionation" title="Disproportionation">disproportionation</a> reactions:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g1275_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-g1275-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_compounds">Chemical compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Chemical compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxygen_compounds">Oxygen compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Oxygen compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Three americium oxides are known, with the oxidation states +2 (AmO), +3 (Am<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>) and +4 (AmO<sub>2</sub>). <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Americium(II)_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Americium(II) oxide (page does not exist)">Americium(II) oxide</a> was prepared in minute amounts and has not been characterized in details.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Americium(III)_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Americium(III) oxide (page does not exist)">Americium(III) oxide</a> is a red-brown solid with a melting point of 2205 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HOWI_1972_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-HOWI_1972-61">[60]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Americium_dioxide" title="Americium dioxide">Americium(IV) oxide</a> is the main form of solid americium which is used in nearly all its applications. As most other actinide dioxides, it is a black solid with a cubic (<a href="/wiki/Fluorite" title="Fluorite">fluorite</a>) crystal structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g1267_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-g1267-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>The oxalate of americium(III), vacuum dried at room temperature, has the chemical formula Am<sub>2</sub>(C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·7H<sub>2</sub>O. Upon heating in vacuum, it loses water at 240 °C and starts decomposing into AmO<sub>2</sub> at 300 °C, the decomposition completes at about 470 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p4_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-p4-47">[46]</a></sup> The initial oxalate dissolves in nitric acid with the maximum solubility of 0.25 g/L.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p5_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-p5-63">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Halides">Halides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Halides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">Halides</a> of americium are known for the oxidation states +2, +3 and +4,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HOWI_1969_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-HOWI_1969-64">[63]</a></sup> where the +3 is most stable, especially in solutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hal1_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-hal1-65">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center;">
<tbody><tr>

<td>

<tr>

</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/Americium(III)_fluoride" title="Americium(III) fluoride">Americium(III) fluoride</a> <br/> AmF<sub>3</sub><br/> pink
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Americium(III)_chloride" title="Americium(III) chloride">Americium(III) chloride</a> <br/> AmCl<sub>3</sub><br/> pink

</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>+2
</th>
<td>
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Americium(II)_chloride" title="Americium(II) chloride">Americium(II) chloride</a> <br/> AmCl<sub>2</sub><br/> black
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Americium(II)_bromide" title="Americium(II) bromide">Americium(II) bromide</a> <br/> AmBr<sub>2</sub><br/> black
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Americium(II)_iodide" title="Americium(II) iodide">Americium(II) iodide</a> <br/> AmI<sub>2</sub><br/> black
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Reduction of Am(III) compounds with sodium <a href="/wiki/Amalgam_(chemistry)" title="Amalgam (chemistry)">amalgam</a> yields Am(II) salts – the black halides AmCl<sub>2</sub>, AmBr<sub>2</sub> and AmI<sub>2</sub>. They are very sensitive to oxygen and oxidize in water, releasing hydrogen and converting back to the Am(III) state. Specific lattice constants are:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Orthorhombic_crystal_system" title="Orthorhombic crystal system">Orthorhombic</a> AmCl<sub>2</sub>: <i>a</i> = <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6990896299999999999♠</span>896.3<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.8 pm</span>, <i>b</i> = <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6990757300000000000♠</span>757.3<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.8 pm</span> and <i>c</i> = <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6990453200000000000♠</span>453.2<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.6 pm</span></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tetragonal_crystal_system" title="Tetragonal crystal system">Tetragonal</a> AmBr<sub>2</sub>: <i>a</i> = <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6991115920000000000♠</span>1<span style="margin-left:.25em;">159</span>.2<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.4 pm</span> and <i>c</i> = <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6990712100000000000♠</span>712.1<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.3 pm</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> They can also be prepared by reacting metallic americium with an appropriate mercury halide HgX<sub>2</sub>, where X = Cl, Br or I:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g1272_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-g1272-67">[66]</a></sup></li></ul>

</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {Am^3+ + 3F^- -&gt; AmF3(v)}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>The tetravalent americium(IV) fluoride (AmF<sub>4</sub>) is obtained by reacting solid americium(III) fluoride with molecular <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-f4_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-f4-68">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g1271_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-g1271-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {2AmF3 + F2 -&gt; 2AmF4}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>

</msubsup>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {2AmF3 + F2 -&gt; 2AmF4}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {2AmF3 + F2 -&gt; 2AmF4}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/902ed696f0a925142b4a9a6c549ce866b28e28a8" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:26.11ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>Another known form of solid tetravalent americium chloride is KAmF<sub>5</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-f4_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-f4-68">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p6_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-p6-70">[69]</a></sup> Tetravalent americium has also been observed in the aqueous phase. For this purpose, black Am(OH)<sub>4</sub> was dissolved in 15-<a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">M</a> NH<sub>4</sub>F with the americium concentration of 0.01 M. The resulting reddish solution had a characteristic optical absorption spectrum which is similar to that of AmF<sub>4</sub> but differed from other oxidation states of americium. Heating the Am(IV) solution to 90 °C did not result in its disproportionation or reduction, however a slow reduction was observed to Am(III) and assigned to self-irradiation of americium by alpha particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amoh4_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-amoh4-51">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most americium(III) halides form hexagonal crystals with slight variation of the color and exact structure between the halogens. So, chloride (AmCl<sub>3</sub>) is reddish and has a structure isotypic to <a href="/wiki/Uranium(III)_chloride" title="Uranium(III) chloride">uranium(III) chloride</a> (space group P6<sub>3</sub>/m) and the melting point of 715 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HOWI_1969_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-HOWI_1969-64">[63]</a></sup> The fluoride is isotypic to LaF<sub>3</sub> (space group P6<sub>3</sub>/mmc) and the iodide to BiI<sub>3</sub> (space group R<span style="text-decoration:overline;">3</span>). The bromide is an exception with the orthorhombic PuBr<sub>3</sub>-type structure and space group Cmcm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hal1_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-hal1-65">[64]</a></sup> Crystals of americium hexahydrate (AmCl<sub>3</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O) can be prepared by dissolving americium dioxide in hydrochloric acid and evaporating the liquid. Those crystals are hygroscopic and have yellow-reddish color and a <a href="/wiki/Monoclinic_crystal_system" title="Monoclinic crystal system">monoclinic</a> crystal structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Oxyhalides of americium in the form Am<sup>VI</sup>O<sub>2</sub>X<sub>2</sub>, Am<sup>V</sup>O<sub>2</sub>X, Am<sup>IV</sup>OX<sub>2</sub> and Am<sup>III</sup>OX can be obtained by reacting the corresponding americium halide with oxygen or Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, and AmOCl can also be produced by vapor phase <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g1272_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-g1272-67">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {AmCl3 + H2O -&gt; AmOCl + 2HCl}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
