<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chalcogenides_and_pnictides">Chalcogenides and pnictides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Chalcogenides and pnictides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The known <a href="/wiki/Chalcogenide" title="Chalcogenide">chalcogenides</a> of americium include the <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfide</a> AmS<sub>2</sub>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM_S_SE_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-AM_S_SE-72">[71]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Selenide" title="Selenide">selenides</a> AmSe<sub>2</sub> and Am<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM_S_SE_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-AM_S_SE-72">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM_METALLIDE_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-AM_METALLIDE-73">[72]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Telluride_(chemistry)" title="Telluride (chemistry)">tellurides</a> Am<sub>2</sub>Te<sub>3</sub> and AmTe<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogen_group" title="Nitrogen group">pnictides</a> of americium (<sup>243</sup>Am) of the AmX type are known for the elements <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a>. They crystallize in the <a href="/wiki/Cubic_crystal_system" title="Cubic crystal system">rock-salt</a> lattice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM_METALLIDE_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-AM_METALLIDE-73">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silicides_and_borides">Silicides and borides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Silicides and borides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Americium <a href="/wiki/Silicide" title="Silicide">monosilicide</a> (AmSi) and "disilicide" (nominally AmSi<sub>x</sub> with: 1.87 &lt; x &lt; 2.0) were obtained by reduction of americium(III) fluoride with elementary <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> in vacuum at 1050 °C (AmSi) and 1150−1200 °C (AmSi<sub>x</sub>). AmSi is a black solid isomorphic with LaSi, it has an orthorhombic crystal symmetry. AmSi<sub>x</sub> has a bright silvery lustre and a tetragonal crystal lattice (space group <i>I</i>4<sub>1</sub>/amd), it is isomorphic with PuSi<sub>2</sub> and ThSi<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Boride" title="Boride">Borides</a> of americium include AmB<sub>4</sub> and AmB<sub>6</sub>. The tetraboride can be obtained by heating an oxide or halide of americium with <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_diboride" title="Magnesium diboride">magnesium diboride</a> in vacuum or inert atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organoamericium_compounds">Organoamericium compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Organoamericium compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranocene-3D-balls.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="891" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Uranocene-3D-balls.png/120px-Uranocene-3D-balls.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Uranocene-3D-balls.png/180px-Uranocene-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Uranocene-3D-balls.png/240px-Uranocene-3D-balls.png 2x" width="120"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranocene-3D-balls.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Predicted structure of amerocene [(η<sup>8</sup>-C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>8</sub>)<sub>2</sub>Am]</div></div></div>
<p>Analogous to <a href="/wiki/Uranocene" title="Uranocene">uranocene</a>, americium forms the organometallic compound amerocene with two <a href="/wiki/Cyclooctatetraene" title="Cyclooctatetraene">cyclooctatetraene</a> ligands, with the chemical formula (η<sup>8</sup>-C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>8</sub>)<sub>2</sub>Am.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Cyclopentadienyl_complex" title="Cyclopentadienyl complex">cyclopentadienyl complex</a> is also known that is likely to be stoichiometrically AmCp<sub>3</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Formation of the complexes of the type Am(n-C<sub>3</sub>H<sub>7</sub>-BTP)<sub>3</sub>, where BTP stands for 2,6-di(1,2,4-triazin-3-yl)pyridine, in solutions containing n-C<sub>3</sub>H<sub>7</sub>-BTP and Am<sup>3+</sup> ions has been confirmed by <a href="/wiki/Extended_X-ray_absorption_fine_structure" title="Extended X-ray absorption fine structure">EXAFS</a>. Some of these BTP-type complexes selectively interact with americium and therefore are useful in its selective separation from lanthanides and another actinides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_aspects">Biological aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Biological aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Americium is an artificial element of recent origin, and thus does not have a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dietary_element" title="Dietary element">biological requirement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> It is harmful to <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a>. It has been proposed to use bacteria for removal of americium and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_(chemistry)" title="Heavy metal (chemistry)">heavy metals</a> from rivers and streams. Thus, <a href="/wiki/Enterobacteriaceae" title="Enterobacteriaceae">Enterobacteriaceae</a> of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Citrobacter" title="Citrobacter">Citrobacter</a></i> precipitate americium ions from aqueous solutions, binding them into a metal-phosphate complex at their cell walls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> Several studies have been reported on the <a href="/wiki/Biosorption" title="Biosorption">biosorption</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">bioaccumulation</a> of americium by bacteria<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> and fungi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fission">Fission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Fission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The isotope <sup>242m</sup>Am (half-life 141 years) has the largest cross sections for absorption of thermal neutrons (5,700 <a href="/wiki/Barn_(unit)" title="Barn (unit)">barns</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karlsruhe_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karlsruhe-89">[88]</a></sup> that results in a small <a href="/wiki/Critical_mass" title="Critical mass">critical mass</a> for a sustained <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chain_reaction" title="Nuclear chain reaction">nuclear chain reaction</a>. The critical mass for a bare <sup>242m</sup>Am sphere is about 9–14 kg (the uncertainty results from insufficient knowledge of its material properties). It can be lowered to 3–5 kg with a metal reflector and should become even smaller with a water reflector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> Such small critical mass is favorable for portable <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a>, but those based on <sup>242m</sup>Am are not known yet, probably because of its scarcity and high price. The critical masses of two other readily available isotopes, <sup>241</sup>Am and <sup>243</sup>Am, are relatively high – 57.6 to 75.6 kg for <sup>241</sup>Am and 209 kg for <sup>243</sup>Am.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-irsn_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-irsn-91">[90]</a></sup> Scarcity and high price yet hinder application of americium as a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel" title="Nuclear fuel">nuclear fuel</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are proposals of very compact 10-kW high-flux reactors using as little as 20 grams of <sup>242m</sup>Am. Such low-power reactors would be relatively safe to use as <a href="/wiki/Neutron_source" title="Neutron source">neutron sources</a> for <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_medicine" title="Nuclear medicine">radiation therapy</a> in hospitals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_americium" title="Isotopes of americium">Isotopes of americium</a></div>
<p>About 19 <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> and 8 <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">nuclear isomers</a> are known for americium. There are two long-lived alpha-emitters, <sup>241</sup>Am and <sup>243</sup>Am with half-lives of 432.2 and 7,370 years, respectively, and the nuclear isomer <sup>242m1</sup>Am has a long half-life of 141 years. The half-lives of other isotopes and isomers range from 0.64 microseconds for <sup>245m1</sup>Am to 50.8 hours for <sup>240</sup>Am. As with most other actinides, the isotopes of americium with odd number of neutrons have relatively high rate of nuclear fission and low critical mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nubase_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-nubase-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Americium-241" title="Americium-241">Americium-241</a> decays to <sup>237</sup>Np emitting alpha particles of 5 different energies, mostly at 5.486 MeV (85.2%) and 5.443 MeV (12.8%). Because many of the resulting states are metastable, they also emit <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a> with the discrete energies between 26.3 and 158.5 keV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Americium-242" title="Americium-242">Americium-242</a> is a short-lived isotope with a half-life of 16.02 h.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nubase_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-nubase-13">[13]</a></sup> It mostly (82.7%) converts by β-decay to <sup>242</sup>Cm, but also by <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a> to <sup>242</sup>Pu (17.3%). Both <sup>242</sup>Cm and <sup>242</sup>Pu transform via nearly the same decay chain through <sup>238</sup>Pu down to <sup>234</sup>U.
</p><p>Nearly all (99.541%) of <sup>242m1</sup>Am decays by <a href="/wiki/Internal_conversion" title="Internal conversion">internal conversion</a> to <sup>242</sup>Am and the remaining 0.459% by α-decay to <sup>238</sup>Np. The latter subsequently decays to <sup>238</sup>Pu and then to <sup>234</sup>U.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nubase_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-nubase-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Americium-243" title="Americium-243">Americium-243</a> transforms by α-emission into <sup>239</sup>Np, which converts by β-decay to <sup>239</sup>Pu, and the <sup>239</sup>Pu changes into <sup>235</sup>U by emitting an α-particle.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Residential_smoke_detector.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="926" data-file-width="903" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Residential_smoke_detector.jpg/200px-Residential_smoke_detector.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Residential_smoke_detector.jpg/300px-Residential_smoke_detector.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Residential_smoke_detector.jpg/400px-Residential_smoke_detector.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:InsideSmokeDetector.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/InsideSmokeDetector.jpg/200px-InsideSmokeDetector.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/InsideSmokeDetector.jpg/300px-InsideSmokeDetector.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/InsideSmokeDetector.jpg/400px-InsideSmokeDetector.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Outside and inside view of an americium-based smoke detector</div></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ionization-type_smoke_detector">Ionization-type smoke detector</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Ionization-type smoke detector">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Smoke_detector#Ionization" title="Smoke detector">Smoke detector § Ionization</a></div>
<p>Americium is used in the most common type of household <a href="/wiki/Smoke_detector" title="Smoke detector">smoke detector</a>, which uses <sup>241</sup>Am in the form of americium dioxide as its source of <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">ionizing radiation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> This isotope is preferred over <sup>226</sup><a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">Ra</a> because it emits 5 times more alpha particles and relatively little harmful gamma radiation. Element collector <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Gray" title="Theodore Gray">Theodore Gray</a> mentions in his book <i>The Elements: A Visual Exploration of Every Known Atom in the Universe</i>:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>You might think that a synthetic radioactive element that follows plutonium (94)—and has a significantly shorter half-life—would be some kind of superbomb material, available only to scientists in secret laboratories. Perhaps a mad scientist is studying americium in a lair somewhere, but if you want some yourself you can simply walk into any neighborhood hardware store, supermarket, or Wal-Mart and buy some, no questions asked.
</p><p>The reason is not that americium is fundamentally less dangerous than the elements around it. In fact, the commonly available isotope, <sup>241</sup>Am, is significantly <i>more</i> radioactive than weapons-grade plutonium, and at least as toxic. No, the difference is simply that there is a useful application for americium that requires only a very tiny amount, and for which a company was prepared to go through the effort required to carve out and get a regulatory exception.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The amount of americium in a typical new smoke detector is 1 <a href="/wiki/Curie" title="Curie">microcurie</a> (37 <a href="/wiki/Becquerel" title="Becquerel">kBq</a>) or 0.29 <a href="/wiki/Microgram" title="Microgram">microgram</a>. This amount declines slowly as the americium decays into <a href="/wiki/Neptunium" title="Neptunium">neptunium</a>-237, a different transuranic element with a much longer half-life (about 2.14 million years). With its half-life of 432.2 years, the americium in a smoke detector includes about 3% <a href="/wiki/Neptunium" title="Neptunium">neptunium</a> after 19 years, and about 5% after 32 years. The radiation passes through an <a href="/wiki/Ionization_chamber" title="Ionization chamber">ionization chamber</a>, an air-filled space between two <a href="/wiki/Electrode" title="Electrode">electrodes</a>, and permits a small, constant <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">current</a> between the electrodes. Any smoke that enters the chamber absorbs the alpha particles, which reduces the ionization and affects this current, triggering the alarm. Compared to the alternative optical smoke detector, the ionization smoke detector is cheaper and can detect particles which are too small to produce significant light scattering; however, it is more prone to <a href="/wiki/Type_I_and_type_II_errors" title="Type I and type II errors">false alarms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIST_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIST-98">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radionuclide">Radionuclide</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Radionuclide">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As <sup>241</sup>Am has a roughly similar half-life to <sup>238</sup>Pu (432.2 years vs. 87 years), it has been proposed as an active element of <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generators</a>, for example in spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RTG_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-RTG-101">[100]</a></sup> Although americium produces less heat and electricity – the power yield is 114.7 mW/g for <sup>241</sup>Am and 6.31 mW/g for <sup>243</sup>Am<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-res_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-res-1">[1]</a></sup> (cf. 390 mW/g for <sup>238</sup>Pu)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RTG_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-RTG-101">[100]</a></sup> – and its radiation poses more threat to humans owing to neutron emission, the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> is considering using americium for its space probes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another proposed space-related application of americium is a fuel for space ships with nuclear propulsion. It relies on the very high rate of nuclear fission of <sup>242m</sup>Am, which can be maintained even in a micrometer-thick foil. Small thickness avoids the problem of self-absorption of emitted radiation. This problem is pertinent to uranium or plutonium rods, in which only surface layers provide alpha-particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rocket_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-rocket-103">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup> The fission products of <sup>242m</sup>Am can either directly propel the spaceship or they can heat a thrusting gas. They can also transfer their energy to a fluid and generate electricity through a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MHD_generator" title="MHD generator">magnetohydrodynamic generator</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mprice_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-mprice-105">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>One more proposal which utilizes the high nuclear fission rate of <sup>242m</sup>Am is a nuclear battery. Its design relies not on the energy of the emitted by americium alpha particles, but on their charge, that is the americium acts as the self-sustaining "cathode". A single 3.2 kg <sup>242m</sup>Am charge of such battery could provide about 140 kW of power over a period of 80 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> Even with all the potential benefits, the current applications of <sup>242m</sup>Am are as yet hindered by the scarcity and high price of this particular <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">nuclear isomer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mprice_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-mprice-105">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neutron_source">Neutron source</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Neutron source">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The oxide of <sup>241</sup>Am pressed with <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">beryllium</a> is an efficient <a href="/wiki/Neutron_source" title="Neutron source">neutron source</a>. Here americium acts as the alpha source, and beryllium produces neutrons owing to its large cross-section for the (α,n) nuclear reaction:
</p>

<p>The most widespread use of <sup>241</sup>AmBe neutron sources is a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_probe" title="Neutron probe">neutron probe</a> – a device used to measure the quantity of water present in soil, as well as moisture/density for quality control in highway construction. <sup>241</sup>Am neutron sources are also used in well logging applications, as well as in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutron_radiography" title="Neutron radiography">neutron radiography</a>, tomography and other radiochemical investigations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Binder_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Binder-107">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_of_other_elements">Production of other elements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Production of other elements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Americium is a starting material for the production of other transuranic elements and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transactinide" title="Transactinide">transactinides</a> – for example, 82.7% of <sup>242</sup>Am decays to <sup>242</sup>Cm and 17.3% to <sup>242</sup>Pu. In the nuclear reactor, <sup>242</sup>Am is also up-converted by neutron capture to <sup>243</sup>Am and <sup>244</sup>Am, which transforms by β-decay to <sup>244</sup>Cm:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {^{243}_{95}Am -&gt;[{\ce {(n,\gamma)}}] ^{244}_{95}Am -&gt;[\beta^-][10.1 \ {\ce {h}}] ^{244}_{96}Cm}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {^{243}_{95}Am -&gt;[{\ce {(n,\gamma)}}] ^{244}_{95}Am -&gt;[\beta^-][10.1 \ {\ce {h}}] ^{244}_{96}Cm}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3ef50e60b621bb8f1eb37a9e5a8f35ce8719fbd4" style="vertical-align: -1.828ex; margin-top: -0.415ex; margin-bottom: -0.51ex; width:32.081ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>Irradiation of <sup>241</sup>Am by <sup>12</sup>C or <sup>22</sup>Ne ions yields the isotopes <sup>247</sup>Es (<a href="/wiki/Einsteinium" title="Einsteinium">einsteinium</a>) or <sup>260</sup>Db (<a href="/wiki/Dubnium" title="Dubnium">dubnium</a>), respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Binder_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Binder-107">[106]</a></sup> Furthermore, the element <a href="/wiki/Berkelium" title="Berkelium">berkelium</a> (<sup>243</sup>Bk isotope) had been first intentionally produced and identified by bombarding <sup>241</sup>Am with alpha particles, in 1949, by the same Berkeley group, using the same 60-inch cyclotron. Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Nobelium" title="Nobelium">nobelium</a> was produced at the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Institute_for_Nuclear_Research" title="Joint Institute for Nuclear Research">Joint Institute for Nuclear Research</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dubna" title="Dubna">Dubna</a>, Russia, in 1965 in several reactions, one of which included irradiation of <sup>243</sup>Am with <sup>15</sup>N ions. Besides, one of the synthesis reactions for <a href="/wiki/Lawrencium" title="Lawrencium">lawrencium</a>, discovered by scientists at Berkeley and Dubna, included bombardment of <sup>243</sup>Am with <sup>18</sup>O.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g1252_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-g1252-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spectrometer">Spectrometer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Spectrometer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Americium-241 has been used as a portable source of both gamma rays and alpha particles for a number of medical and industrial uses. The 59.5409 keV gamma ray emissions from <sup>241</sup>Am in such sources can be used for indirect analysis of materials in <a href="/wiki/Radiography" title="Radiography">radiography</a> and <a href="/wiki/X-ray_fluorescence" title="X-ray fluorescence">X-ray fluorescence</a> spectroscopy, as well as for quality control in fixed <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_density_gauge" title="Nuclear density gauge">nuclear density gauges</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_densometer" title="Nuclear densometer">nuclear densometers</a>. For example, the element has been employed to gauge <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a> thickness to help create flat glass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g1262_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-g1262-23">[23]</a></sup> Americium-241 is also suitable for calibration of gamma-ray spectrometers in the low-energy range, since its spectrum consists of nearly a single peak and negligible Compton continuum (at least three orders of magnitude lower intensity).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup> Americium-241 gamma rays were also used to provide passive diagnosis of thyroid function. This medical application is however obsolete.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_concerns">Health concerns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Americium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Health concerns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As a highly radioactive element, americium and its compounds must be handled only in an appropriate laboratory under special arrangements. Although most americium isotopes predominantly emit alpha particles which can be blocked by thin layers of common materials, many of the daughter products emit gamma-rays and neutrons which have a long penetration depth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>If consumed, most of the americium is excreted within a few days, with only 0.05% absorbed in the blood, of which roughly 45%  goes to the <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> and 45% to the bones, and the remaining 10% is excreted. The uptake to the liver depends on the individual and increases with age. In the bones, americium is first deposited over <a href="/wiki/Cortex_(anatomy)" title="Cortex (anatomy)">cortical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trabecula" title="Trabecula">trabecular</a> surfaces and slowly redistributes over the bone with time. The biological half-life of <sup>241</sup>Am is 50 years in the bones and 20 years in the liver, whereas in the <a href="/wiki/Gonad" title="Gonad">gonads</a> (testicles and ovaries) it remains permanently; in all these organs, americium promotes formation of cancer cells as a result of its radioactivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-am_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-am-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>Americium often enters landfills from discarded <a href="/wiki/Smoke_detector" title="Smoke detector">smoke detectors</a>. The rules associated with the disposal of smoke detectors are relaxed in most jurisdictions. In 1994, 17-year-old <a href="/wiki/David_Hahn" title="David Hahn">David Hahn</a> extracted the americium from about 100 smoke detectors in an attempt to build a breeder nuclear reactor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Silverstein2005_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Silverstein2005-112">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> There have been a few cases of exposure to americium, the worst case being that of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_technologist" title="Chemical technologist">chemical operations technician</a> <a href="/wiki/Harold_McCluskey" title="Harold McCluskey">Harold McCluskey</a>, who at the age of 64 was exposed to 500 times the occupational standard for americium-241 as a result of an explosion in his lab. McCluskey died at the age of 75 of unrelated pre-existing disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tristateherald_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-tristateherald-116">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
